<div id="review_2477985787" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2477985787" />
    <a title="Jeannette Nikolova" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2574284-jeannette-nikolova"><img alt="Jeannette Nikolova" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480677087p2/2574284.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2477985787?book_show_action=true">Dec 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeannette Nikolova" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeannette Nikolova" href="/user/show/2574284-jeannette-nikolova">Jeannette Nikolova</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2477985787" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11805686773698985502"><a href='https://wondrousbooks.wordpress.com/2018/12/10/saatleri-ayarlama-enstitusu-by-ahmed-hamdi-tanpinar/' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <i>Also available on the WondrousBooks blog.</i>
</a><br/><br/><strong>Saatleri Ayarlama Enstitusu</strong> (<em>The Time Regulation Institute</em>) is a book I've wanted to read for a very long time. While the description suggests nothing of the sort, ever since I heard the title, I felt as if there was something otherworldly or fantastical about it.<br/><br/>I was both completely wrong and somewhat right.<br/><br/><strong>Saatleri Ayarlama Enstitusu</strong> is the most Kafkaesque Turkish book I've come in contact with and it is every bit as stuffy and absurd as, say, <strong>The </strong></span>
  <span id="freeText11805686773698985502" style="display:none"><a href="https://wondrousbooks.wordpress.com/2018/12/10/saatleri-ayarlama-enstitusu-by-ahmed-hamdi-tanpinar/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Also available on the WondrousBooks blog.</i>
</a><br /><br /><strong>Saatleri Ayarlama Enstitusu</strong> (<em>The Time Regulation Institute</em>) is a book I've wanted to read for a very long time. While the description suggests nothing of the sort, ever since I heard the title, I felt as if there was something otherworldly or fantastical about it.<br /><br />I was both completely wrong and somewhat right.<br /><br /><strong>Saatleri Ayarlama Enstitusu</strong> is the most Kafkaesque Turkish book I've come in contact with and it is every bit as stuffy and absurd as, say, <strong>The Trial</strong>. The book is the memoir of <em>Hayri Irdal</em>, a man out of time, who takes on the task of regulating time. His narrative starts with his youth and all the people, and clocks, that influenced him, and goes through his introductions to <em>Dr Ramiz</em> and <em>Halit Ayarci</em>, finally arriving to the creation of the Time Regulation Institute. Hayri Irdal's journey is confusing, possibly recounted by an unreliable narrator, and certainly by a very unpleasant one.<br /><br />Hayri Irdal is the embodiment of backwards thinking in the progressive world of Ataturk's Republic. Try as he may, he is unable to feel at ease with the modern world. Even though his professional task is to help set all the watches and clocks in Turkey to the Western time (a 1926 act by Ataturk), his personality fights against that same modern time. From his very first stories it is apparent that the world he wants to belong in is that of the old Istanbul - the men, drinking their raki, the women taking care of the children and the family nest, - and not the world in which everyone wants to shine in the society, where his friends have studied abroad about baffling subjects such as psychology, and where his wife is so keen on living a rich, lavish life, that she makes one up for both of them.<br /><br />Dr Ramiz, on the contrary, is the figure that represents Western progress in both culture and science, and Hayri Irdal is often convinced that that man is very peculiar, with his psychology and attempts in psychoanalysis. Both Dr Ramiz and Halit Ayarci are total opposites of what Hayri Irdal wants - Dr Ramiz represents the scientific development, while Halit Ayarci is embodiment of the new upper class, which has the charm, the money, the courage and the bright ideas. Ultimately, both men are admired and at the same time, frowned upon by Hayri Irdal. And while he presents some of their characteristics as highly unlikable in any sense, I would also argue that Hayri Irdal is the type of narrator who doesn't manage to build enough trust, similarly to what I would think if someone told me chocolate milk was made from brown cows (which, as it turns out, is a belief that 7% of American adults harbour). Simultaneously, Hayri Irdal is also the most passively destructive person I've read about in a long time. He is so selfish, so backwards and keen on staying so, that he wants no part in being a responsible adult, or the head of a family, instead leaving his wives and children to try to sort the world out for themselves, while Hayri Irdal himself wallows in self-pity for this world he can't find a place in and looks for one at the bottom of a bottle.<br /><br />The entire process of Hayri Irdal trying to figure the changing times, the time regulation, and his change of role in society, is a very disheartening reading experience. It's dry, slow and dragged out, while also being very dreary. Very similarly to the repeated struggles of Kafka's Josef K., this book also shows a dark world of pointless labour, bureaucracy and utter absurdism.<br /><br />While I would firmly state that I see why this book is a well-beloved classic, which fully displays the ever ongoing issue of modernism vs classical values in Turkey, I just don't think it's my type of a book. From my point of view, and having grown up, and now continuing to live in an Eastern bloc country, I would say that I have too much of absurdist bureaucracy and mind-numbing backwardness, even if I don't add more to my literary life.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11805686773698985502" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1876536032" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1876536032" />
    <a title="Thomas Hübner" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23833484-thomas-h-bner"><img alt="Thomas Hübner" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1378834728p2/23833484.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1876536032?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas Hübner" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas Hübner" href="/user/show/23833484-thomas-h-bner">Thomas Hübner</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143106739" href="/book/show/13585696-the-time-regulation-institute">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1876536032" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1229562122155668523"><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.mytwostotinki.com/?p=3374'>http://www.mytwostotinki.com/?p=3374</a> <br/><br/>Years ago, I came across the name of Ahmet Hamdi Tanpinar (1901-1962) for the first time. I was reading Orhan Pamuk’s book about Istanbul and Pamuk refers to Tanpinar as his most important teacher as a writer and novelist. That’s a sufficient reason to have a closer look at this author and his novel The Time Regulation Institute, first published in book form in 1962.<br/><br/>Tanpinar belongs to a generation of Turkish authors that grew up in the Ottoman Empire and liv</span>
  <span id="freeText1229562122155668523" style="display:none"><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.mytwostotinki.com/?p=3374">http://www.mytwostotinki.com/?p=3374</a> <br /><br />Years ago, I came across the name of Ahmet Hamdi Tanpinar (1901-1962) for the first time. I was reading Orhan Pamuk’s book about Istanbul and Pamuk refers to Tanpinar as his most important teacher as a writer and novelist. That’s a sufficient reason to have a closer look at this author and his novel The Time Regulation Institute, first published in book form in 1962.<br /><br />Tanpinar belongs to a generation of Turkish authors that grew up in the Ottoman Empire and lived through the first decades of modern Turkey with its Kemalist reforms that deeply affected every aspect of life. It is important to keep in mind that – while the book is clearly a modern novel, written by a university professor that was very familiar with modern European literature – the author was at the same time deeply rooted in the pre-modern Ottoman traditions; and the same is true for his characters in The Time Regulation Institute.<br /><br />The novel is the first-person narrative of the life of Hayri Irdal, a loafer, a man who grows up in rather poor circumstances and with limited school education, but who shows at an early age a talent in repairing watches, a craft he learns at the workshop of Nuri Efendi, whose thoughts about the role of time and about how important it is to make good use of it will play an important role later in the novel.<br /><br />We see Hayri Irdal being a rather weak person, with a problematic second marriage (after his first wife died early) and with children that are not really close to him. He is spending most of his time with a strange circle of friends, alchemists, spiritualists, fortune seekers, project makers. This collection of characters gives Tanpinar an opportunity to unfold his satirical talents. The first part of the novel takes place in the time before and during WWI, and this part frequently reminded me of a Karagöz performance, the Turkish version of the Commedia dell’arte; the tales of Nasreddin Hoxha came to mind as well. Not only the men are shown as objects of the author’s wit, also the female characters get their fair share of satirical treatment, particularly Hamdi’s aunt, but also his second wife and her sisters with their obsessions regarding cinema or singing.<br /><br />A change of luck for Hayri happens in the moment when he gets acquainted with Doctor Ramiz, a psychoanalytic who has just returned from Vienna and who applies his newly acquired (and superficial) knowledge of modern psychotherapy to cure his patient, with analysis of dreams that the doctor “orders” his patient to have, and discussion of German-language brochures on psychoanalysis inclusive (Hayri speaks of course only Turkish). Once Hayri is released from the hospital, Doctor Ramiz introduces him to Halit Ayarci, a modern project maker who understands to utilize Hayri’s potential and who – thanks to his connections in important circles – is also able to find the funding for a revolutionary idea: the creation of the Time Regulation Institute. The institute, a (fictitious) part of the reforms in the 1920’s in Turkey is supposed to ensure that all clocks and watches in the country show the correct time, and a mechanism to ensure that – and of course also a sophisticated system of fines – is quickly developed.<br /><br />A big part of the second half of the novel deals with the finding of financial backing of the project, the creation of a bureaucracy and the erection of a suitable and representative office building of the new institute, so that the big number of employees – all of them of course relatives and friends of the director, Halit Ayarci, and his deputy, Hayri Irdal – have excellent working conditions. A special task assigned to Hayri is the writing of the biography of Ahmet Zamani Efendi, an Ottoman predecessor of the idea of measuring time in the modern way; and the fact that this person never existed gives Hayri a perfect opportunity to bring his storytelling talent to good use. Too bad that a Western scholar shows up one day, who is looking for further evidence, and that on top of it, a visiting commission questions the work and the usefulness of the Time Regulation Institute…<br /><br />Bureaucracies seem to have a great fascination for novelists. But while Kafka or Ismail Kadare (in his Palace of Dreams) focus on the dark, nightmarish implications of such bureaucratic institutions, Tanpinar offers his readers the satirical version, a farce. Hayri represents the old generation of people who don’t really believe in what the institute stands for, but who seize the opportunity to employ a lot of friends and relatives, or who take advantage of it in any other way; Halit Ayarci on the other hand is a ‘modern‘ character, someone who clearly understands that the institute can be an instrument to satisfy his personal ambition, and who is very clever in using his contacts in political circles to find the money and public recognition for his project. And of course, a bank and a housing project are also needed in this context…<br /><br />I mentioned already that Tanpinar was very familiar with modern European literature; and I can’t help but thinking about Italo Svevo’s Zeno Cosini, or the “heroes” of the novels of Robert Walser that seem to come from the same mould as Tanpinar’s characters. Halit Ayarci and his project also reminded me a bit of the Parallelaktion, and the character of Arnheim in Robert Musil’s Man without Qualities.<br /><br />What is the message of Tanpinar’s novel? Maybe this one: you cannot transform people that are deeply rooted in a medieval society into modernity just by enforcing some radical reforms, like the Kemalists did in the 1920’s and 1930’s. All these reforms will be superficial and will not change the mindset of people. Tanpinar mentions the paintings of Osman Hamdi Bey, and the tortoise of Hayri’s dervish friend is also an obvious reference to the most famous of Osman’s paintings, The Tortoise Instructor; the tortoises being a symbol for the Turkish people as seen by Osman. Can you really teach or instruct a tortoise? Tanpinar ends on a slightly more optimistic note. Hayri’s estranged son is supporting his father in the end with his construction project; and while he clearly sees his father’s limitations – and that of his surrounding -, this son who distanced himself geographically, and also by name (choosing a new name for himself), seems to look at his father with mild irony and understanding. Several generations need to pass probably until a modern Turkish society will evolve. In the meantime, Hayri, and also Turkish society in general still struggle with the Father complex that was diagnosed by Doctor Ramiz in the novel. But I am quite sure, Tanpinar had not only Turkey in mind when he wrote about bureaucracies, and about how a certain category of men is using projects like the Time Regulation Institute to re-write or plainly invent the past and turn such projects into a kind of machinery for self-promotion, generation of media attention, influence, and money. Therefore, it is easy to relate to this novel, even when the reader may not be familiar with the Turkish setting and background.<br /><br />Overall, I can say that I liked and enjoyed this book very much. It is a fun read thanks to the ability of its author to combine satirical criticism, traditional ‘oriental’ storytelling, and a very accomplished use of the form of the modern novel, with an unreliable narrator. If Tanpinar had lived longer and would have had an opportunity to edit his novel more diligently, he might have cut some passages that are redundant, but that’s a very small criticism of an otherwise truly important and enjoyable book.<br /><br />The translators of this edition made an obvious effort to render the author’s multi-layered style into a similarly multi-layered English. I cannot really judge the quality of the translation since I don’t know Turkish, but it was a smooth read that didn’t create any challenges for me as a reader.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1229562122155668523" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1169505353" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1169505353" />
    <a title="Paul Fulcher" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3250759-paul-fulcher"><img alt="Paul Fulcher" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542271295p2/3250759.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1169505353?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Fulcher" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Fulcher" href="/user/show/3250759-paul-fulcher">Paul Fulcher</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781892381033" href="/book/show/3148298-the-time-regulation-institute">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3250759-paul-fulcher?shelf=2015">2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1169505353" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10243439196652557640">The Time Regulation Institute illustrates well both the joys, and some of the frustrations, of reading translated literature.<br/><br/>The joy is in discovering such rich novels, unlike anything written in English, and which both inform the British reader on aspects of world cultures but also provide telling insights into our own.<br/><br/>Against that, this novel comes, by necessity, with an introduction to explain the historical context, a translator's foreword, an appendix with a description of Turkish names and</span>
  <span id="freeText10243439196652557640" style="display:none">The Time Regulation Institute illustrates well both the joys, and some of the frustrations, of reading translated literature.<br /><br />The joy is in discovering such rich novels, unlike anything written in English, and which both inform the British reader on aspects of world cultures but also provide telling insights into our own.<br /><br />Against that, this novel comes, by necessity, with an introduction to explain the historical context, a translator's foreword, an appendix with a description of Turkish names and honorifics including various untranslatable puns used in the characters name, and three pages of notes. <br /><br />And the praiseworthy translators, Maureen Freely, best known as the main translator of Orhan Pamuk, and Alexander Dawe, admit that there are some features of the original they simply can't capture directly. For example, in the original, the language of the narrator slips out of the approved modern Turkish, created by Ataturk's Turkish Language Society, into old fashioned Ottoman, and as they say:<blockquote>Its hero, Hayri Irdal, speaks a language that, however much it strains to keep pace with modern times, keeps collapsing into its old ways. As much as he tries to embrace new words and ideas, his old ones come back to claim him. This losing battle is evident in Hayri's every reminiscence. Guilelessly he climbs from clause to clause, as we count the seconds before the edifice starts to teeter.<br /><br />That's how it is, at least, for those of us who have had the privilige of reading The Time Regulation Institute in the original.</blockquote>Ataturk's modernisation program for Turkey is very much the inspiration for The Time Regulation Institute, a fictional institution created to regulate timepieces across the country, and indeed to promote the use of watches and clocks, in order to improve efficiency.<br /><br />However, the Time Regulation Institute is not some Orwellian parable, but rather a riotous celebration of life and society. It's main note is absurdist humour.<br /><br />The book's narrator, Hayri Irdal doesn't even meet the key other character, Halit Ayarci, who establishes the Institute, until halfway through the novel. Instead the first 200 pages tell, via a highly digressive (almost Tristram Shandyesque) narration, the story of Hayri's life to that point, largely a series of tragicomic misadventures. He justifies his editorial decision:<blockquote>Why have I burdened my chronicle of the Time Regulation Institute with these distant reveries....However I might regret it, I cannot explain myself without looking back...it is my past, and not my current position in life, that holds the key to my problems; I can neither escape from it nor entirely accept it's mandate</blockquote>After this point, the book focuses on the Time Regulation Institute, but still considerable pages are devoted to the affairs of Hayri's extended family and acquaintances, and this was to my mind the weakest aspect of the novel; the expansive cast of characters is rather confusing and their stories get in the way of the main plot - it's as if . I found myself skim reading or even skipping some sections.<br /><br />In contrast, the tale of the Time Regulation Institute sees the book reach satrical heights. The author's target is wider than just Kemalism, but bureaucratic practices generally.<br /><br />The Institute starts of with a statistical study of how different groups of society manage their time, except a staff member first draws graphs of the results, then the Director, Halit Ayarci, decides on what they mean, and the actual collection of any data seems rather unnecessary. Hayri is perplexed:<blockquote>"But, sir," I said, "shouldn't this be done in just the opposite way? That is to say, first a survey is conducted, and then the figures, or rather the results, are collected. Then columns that represent these results are arranged accordingly. At least that's as much as I know about ---"<br /><br />Halit Ayarci looked at me as if for the first time.<br /><br />"The old method," he said. "Obsolete and absurd, and it gobbles up a terrible amount of time, offering no conclusive evidence. This method is far more reliable. Here the margin of error is reduced as there's no possibility for any verification."</blockquote>Hayri's first task as an employee is to write an inspirational biography of the famous Ahmet the Timely, an eminent 17th Century Turkish scholar of clocks and time, who made mathematical breakthroughs well before Western mathematicians made the same results famous. But Hayri sees one problem - Ahmet was actually invented off the cuff by Halit Ayarci to impress some visiting dignitaries.<blockquote>Now it was Halit Ayarci's turn to be indignant: "You don't even believe in Ahmet the Timely"<br /><br />"Well, that's because there's no such man. He's simply not there. There's not a trace of such a man in all history! Show me one single document, just a mention of the name - that's enough."<br /><br />Dr Ramiz interjected: "That way of thinking is antiquated. History is at the disposal of the present."</blockquote>When the "biography" is published it meets generally with great acclaim, but some hostile reviewers point out apparent contradictions and impossibilities in the account. Hayri is concerned that they've made mistakes but the Director disagrees conceptually:<blockquote>Mistakes only exist for those foolish enough to fix them. But we are different! Once we have accepted an error, we rise above it. But no, Hayri Bey, oh no, there's no such thing as a mistake and there never will be. It's all a matter of solid preparation and faith in man's potential</blockquote>Eventually Hayri comes to accept the Director's way of thinking and in particular embraces the self-perpetuating Institute as a way to create jobs for his friends and families. Once word gets around:<blockquote>I was amazed to discover just how large my family was. I was likewise impressed by the loyalty of old school friends and the continuing affection that my old neighbourhood acquaintances still had for me....Most were people sensitive enough to stay at arm's length during the years when my spirits had been so dampened by misfortune; now they had entered into an all-out war as they struggled to give me the chance to prove that I too felt compassion for my friends and relatives</blockquote>Even when disaster seemingly strikes, the press and politicians realise the absurd nature of the Institute. ("<i>They condemned the organisation for its unwieldy size and inefficiency. Overlooking the fact that we created jobs for so many in a country where unemployment is rampant, they railed against our excesses: three management offices, eleven management branches, forty-seven typists, and two hundred seventy control bureaus.</i>") The order comes to dissolve it, Halit Ayarci still manages to save the day for the key employees:<blockquote>"I have had the decision amended. That's to say, the decision to abrogate had been suspended, but in order for the institute to remain in a permanent state of liquidation we need to form a committee for continuous liquidation. All our friends will have positions in this new entity."</blockquote>Overall, a worthwhile andn very enjoyable read, but only three stars due to both the higher than usual loss (in use of language and cultural references) from the translation into English, notwithstanding the translators heroic efforts, and the rather distracting side story of the family and friends.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10243439196652557640" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1333862453" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1333862453" />
    <a title="Cărăşălu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5885579-c-r-lu"><img alt="Cărăşălu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1345808900p2/5885579.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1333862453?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cărăşălu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cărăşălu" href="/user/show/5885579-c-r-lu">Cărăşălu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143106739" href="/book/show/13585696-the-time-regulation-institute">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1333862453" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6713148083303938062">The book is smart, full of humour and nostalgia, and a narrative style I haven't read in a while, where words flow, sentences are long and paragraphs even longer. It makes fun of modern music, bureaucracy, mass media and PR, civil society and also of old-fashioned misfits.<br/><br/>It was initially hard for me to get to care about the story dealing the early miserable and unfortunate life of Hayri Irdal, but it gets considerably better when he meets Halit Ayarci, one of the best characters I've encountere</span>
  <span id="freeText6713148083303938062" style="display:none">The book is smart, full of humour and nostalgia, and a narrative style I haven't read in a while, where words flow, sentences are long and paragraphs even longer. It makes fun of modern music, bureaucracy, mass media and PR, civil society and also of old-fashioned misfits.<br /><br />It was initially hard for me to get to care about the story dealing the early miserable and unfortunate life of Hayri Irdal, but it gets considerably better when he meets Halit Ayarci, one of the best characters I've encountered in recently read books, the epitome of a modern man, a man of action, both cynical and optimist. Very intelligent, a gifted schemer, a charming speaker, he even seams a charlatan at times, but he definitely isn't. Not in a simple way, anyhow.<br /><br />Why a successful man like him pairs up with our narrator, Hayri, who seems like a born loser? By the end of the book, Halit confesses that Hayri made him love life again, but I won't spoil anymore details.<br /><br />It's in their juxtaposition that we get to understand Hayri better and why his life had been so sad until meeting Halit. His fault is simple: old-school common sense, which becomes obsolete in modernity. Hayri difficulties to cope with the times, which he finds utterly absurd, reminded me of Catch-22's Yossarian, who finds himself in equally absurd situations.<br /><br />Meanwhile, modern Halit has no trouble in mixing up truth with illusions, reality with imagination, exploiting the early modernity's chaotic rules or lack thereof in his quest to build a huge bureaucratic monster to embody the new age: <br /><br />"I would go as far as to say that it is an age in which bureaucracy has reached its zenith, an age of real freedom. Any man who understands is a valuable figure. I am in the process of establishing an absolute institution - a mechanism that defines its own function. What could be closer to perfection than that?". That is, an institution with no actual usefulness or externally-defined purpose, but one that is creating and imposing its own self-defined meaning. Much like most modern NGOs and companies.<br /><br />And so the two of them build the Time Regulation Institute - an organization doing... basically nothing. It's Hayri's struggle to make sense of his new job that makes the story so good: the Institute is essentially a parody of a modern NGO and company of the "too big to fail" type. In fact, Halit expressly states that they must have as many employees as possible in order to attract more funding and their jobs will figure themselves out naturally. <br /><br />Hayri is initially skeptical, being old-fashioned he would prefer a real job, a meaningful one, but he cannot give up the new-found well-being achieved thanks to the Institute. He learns that all his moral, ethical, practical rules are no good today, when everything works the other way around.<br /><br />When Hayri asks for truth, Halit tells him "Being a realist does not mean seeing the truth for what it is. It is a question of determining our relationship with the truth in the way that it is the most beneficial for us". And he goes even further: "Knowledge holds us back. Indeed it offers neither an end nor an aim. The main thing is to do, to create. If they only knew, if they knew... But if they knew, they wouldn't be doing it. They'd never achieve the same innovation, the same excitement at spontaneous discovery. Knowledge would stifle it all".<br /><br />When Hayri judges his sister-in-law's musical aspirations, he judges her by classical standards and deems her horrible, but Halit thinks these very things, her lack of training and talent in a traditional sense, make her "original and relatable" and indeed she becomes a star. <br /><br />One of the funny moments when Hayri has to design a new building for the Institute and, in order to make it conceptually appropriate and modern, he designs a kind of architectural bullshit which gets praised as an innovation and wins him several awards, but no architect actually endeavours to oversee the construction process. Moreover, everyone is horrified when Hayri is proposed by Halit to design their new homes. <br /><br />As he points well, "They were sincer on both occasions. They adore innovation providing it doesn't affect them personally. And they continue to adore it but with one condition. In their personal lives they prefer to be safe and secure". Oh, how often have I witnessed this in real life, in matters much more trivial such as computer programs.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6713148083303938062" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carolinemawer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carolinemawer" href="/user/show/18262460-carolinemawer">Carolinemawer</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781892381033" href="/book/show/3148298-the-time-regulation-institute">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer984827026" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14523677288389870825">I loved this book. <br/>Its very funny, hugely absorbing and has many more layers than I suspect I can imagine. <br/>I'd like to pay especial tribute to the translators - their description of their translating gives the tiniest of clues into how even more rewarding it would have been to read this book in the original.<br/>In translation, the smallness and nigh-on-simultaneous largeness of the lives of the people in this book are, for me, a big part of why it is such a great story. The book is billed as an "all</span>
  <span id="freeText14523677288389870825" style="display:none">I loved this book. <br />Its very funny, hugely absorbing and has many more layers than I suspect I can imagine. <br />I'd like to pay especial tribute to the translators - their description of their translating gives the tiniest of clues into how even more rewarding it would have been to read this book in the original.<br />In translation, the smallness and nigh-on-simultaneous largeness of the lives of the people in this book are, for me, a big part of why it is such a great story. The book is billed as an "allegory of the collision of tradition and modernity", as sending up the obsession with grabbing a concocted future in early nationalist Turkey. And it does all that. But we all have similarly minute details looming over us in overwhelming ways; we all dream of changing the world in fundamental ways; we all have dreamy successes and nightmare failures. We're all out of control for more of the time than we care to admit. Just like in the book. <br />As I get older, I'm more and more convinced that nothing really new happens. Also that there's nothing really 'foreign', if you do but open your eyes. <br />So I think this book applies to everyone in all times. </span>

<div id="review_1556399044" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1556399044" />
    <a title="Timons Esaias" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32413649-timons-esaias"><img alt="Timons Esaias" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1403230475p2/32413649.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1556399044?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Timons Esaias" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Timons Esaias" href="/user/show/32413649-timons-esaias">Timons Esaias</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143106739" href="/book/show/13585696-the-time-regulation-institute">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1556399044" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9199967923528000431">It took me quite a while to finish this novel -- I kept having to put it aside to finish something else -- so I didn't get as much out of the experience as I could have. The book could also use a dramatis personae list, because the Turkish names don't remind you of family relationships, and so the names often failed to remind me fully of who the character was in relation to others. (Several folks get married and divorced, and then marry somebody else, so I really should have taken notes...if I h</span>
  <span id="freeText9199967923528000431" style="display:none">It took me quite a while to finish this novel -- I kept having to put it aside to finish something else -- so I didn't get as much out of the experience as I could have. The book could also use a dramatis personae list, because the Turkish names don't remind you of family relationships, and so the names often failed to remind me fully of who the character was in relation to others. (Several folks get married and divorced, and then marry somebody else, so I really should have taken notes...if I had known.)<br /><br />The Introduction is unclear about where and how this book was originally published, giving the idea that it first appeared in 1962, the year Tanpinar died (in January). Online sources reveal that it was serialized starting in 1954, and was first collected in book form in 1961.<br /><br />I suspect that I missed some of the satire, not knowing details of Turkish life and the time when Atatürk issued the Gregorian Calendar Act. Still, the book feels rather like soft-Bulgakov, and had some of the conceits of The Glass Bead Game. It certainly speaks to the Trump era, for it places facts well behind message when it comes to public life.<br /><br />This book has lots of delightful lines and droll observations. "As my teachers saw less of me, they saw fewer of my flaws." "I have tripped over time." (Which is the theme of the book, really.) And there's the very 1984-ish response to a complaint about something being a complete fraud: "That way of thinking is antiquated. History is at the disposal of the present. I can show you hundreds of papers on hundreds of topics, and they are all lies, so what's the difference?" "They adore innovation as long as it doesn't affect them personally."<br /><br />I liked the novel, despite the rocky trip getting through it. I'm likely to read his other translated novel, A Mind at Peace, at some point. I find myself agreeing with Orhan Pamuk's comments on Tanpinar's importance, and wish I'd run across him years ago. This could certainly be included in a college syllabus for European Literature, or 20th Century Novels.<br /><br />One complaint about the translation, or the editing, whichever gave rise to the solecisms "stepped foot" and "free reign" which both occur in the text. It's embarrassing to see them in a Penguin Edition, but we live in degraded times.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9199967923528000431" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1701760295" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1701760295" />
    <a title="Catalina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5481595-catalina"><img alt="Catalina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1533295919p2/5481595.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1701760295?book_show_action=true">Jul 18, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Catalina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Catalina" href="/user/show/5481595-catalina">Catalina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143106739" href="/book/show/13585696-the-time-regulation-institute">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5481595-catalina?shelf=middle-eastern">middle-eastern</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1701760295" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer545889656410969878">Reading The Time Regulation Institute was an almost cathartic experience: excruciating at times but relieving in the end, contributing to one's understanding of life's works. The novel portrays the absurdities of Turkish bureaucratic system and society, at large, from the perspective of the narrator, Hayri Irdal. The narrator, at the same time the main character seems to be the only one who perceives as absurd the manner in which things are done around him. Towards the end of the novel, it can b</span>
  <span id="freeText545889656410969878" style="display:none">Reading The Time Regulation Institute was an almost cathartic experience: excruciating at times but relieving in the end, contributing to one's understanding of life's works. The novel portrays the absurdities of Turkish bureaucratic system and society, at large, from the perspective of the narrator, Hayri Irdal. The narrator, at the same time the main character seems to be the only one who perceives as absurd the manner in which things are done around him. Towards the end of the novel, it can be argued that his role model, Halit Ayarci might have had the same perspective, but never talked about it. <br />Concerning the style in which the novel was written, although it might be difficult to the western reader to follow, the paragraphs that can spread on pages on end are indicative of the Oriental storytelling style. This can be observed in the way Hayri narrates the story, but also in how some other characters talk: for example, when Nermin Hanim starts telling her life story she goes off on many tangents as well. However, when he notices these derivations present in other people's storytelling, Hayri seems to be more critical of the style. Despite Hayri acknowledging these practices and events as illogical and absurd, he does not relate them with irony, but with surprise and maybe curiosity for human nature.<br />If the reader can follow the plot line, the story is not necessarily an extraordinary one for the time in Turkey: times of change provided people with no education and/or talent to go up the socio-economic ladder. The way this was reluctantly accomplished for Hayri Bey was through luck and social connections. But more than simply telling Hayri's life story, the novel is indicative of the times and social practices of the beginnings of Turkey. Overall, the novel is an enchanting one, even with its countless characters and tangents.</span>
  <a data-text-id="545889656410969878" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText13650462433521769884" style="display:none">The time regulation institute is so complex and deep that I don't think I will be able to write a comprehensive review how can i ? it's a turkish classic which takes you back in time. <br /><br />This classic is a masterpiece.I will certainly never read something like this ever. It is complex because of the numerous subplots and simultaneous thoughts which were going in my head while reading it.<br /><br />On one hand it deals with Phycology, social injustice, dark humor, mockery, culture and many more aspects and the early 20th century modern state of turkey. Hayri's, our patrogonist's father phobia and his poor life. The pain which he felt throughout his life is so deep that he started cursing and blaming a wall clock for that. And later on when he is sent to a medical centre because of a theft of imaginary diamonds, he didn't stole.<br /><br />On the other hand it deals with the price being paid by eastern Europe for late industrialization and the pressure of coping up with the west and western regulations. It clearly indicated that the land which are connected, cannot function without some sort of common regulation and thus the reforms and regulations one such regulation was and is the turkish time which follows european standards. And also it showed the changes in language, rules that modern state of turkey adopted from europe. In short the transition. <br /><br />I lived in turkey for a year and I read this book there I don't think I would have been able to understand it, if I wasn't living there it is such a deep and remarkable book not just for learning turkish but middle eastern culture of that time.<br />This book has it all history, culture, economic, phychology you just name it and you will find it in this masterpiece. <br /><br /><br /></span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2183122993?book_show_action=true">Nov 17, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Murtaza " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Murtaza " href="/user/show/13716226-murtaza">Murtaza </a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2183122993" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10327188434289816332">A darkly comic novel about traditional Turkish society being forced into the labyrinth of bureaucratic modernity. I first heard about it after reading an excellent <a href='https://www.theguardian.com/books/2015/feb/28/ahmet-hamdi-tanpinar-the-time-regulation-institute-turkey-western-time' rel='nofollow noopener'>Pankaj Mishra</a> review about it a few years ago, although this was a novel written a half century ago. The plot deals with a fictional organization called the Time Regulation Institute created by Republican Turkey to help synchronize all the clocks in the country, and to teach Turks how to live as modern people in the process. The main </span>
  <span id="freeText10327188434289816332" style="display:none">A darkly comic novel about traditional Turkish society being forced into the labyrinth of bureaucratic modernity. I first heard about it after reading an excellent <a href="https://www.theguardian.com/books/2015/feb/28/ahmet-hamdi-tanpinar-the-time-regulation-institute-turkey-western-time" rel="nofollow noopener">Pankaj Mishra</a> review about it a few years ago, although this was a novel written a half century ago. The plot deals with a fictional organization called the Time Regulation Institute created by Republican Turkey to help synchronize all the clocks in the country, and to teach Turks how to live as modern people in the process. The main character, Hayri Irdal, is very much like Dostoyevsky's quietly tormented protagonist from Notes from the Underground, the quintessential lonely man swept up by the forces of modernity. The book deals with the absurdities of modern bureaucracy and the mandates that Turks were given to craft a new history and identity for themselves by fiat.<br /><br />As a novel the book was a little bit disjointed in its style, but it overcomes this by frequently being hilarious. I'm sure its even better in the original Turkish, but the melancholy and humor still survives quite well in this version.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10327188434289816332" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert Wechsler" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert Wechsler" href="/user/show/17447518-robert-wechsler">Robert Wechsler</a>
      </span>

          marked it as tasted


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781892381033" href="/book/show/3148298-the-time-regulation-institute">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17447518-robert-wechsler?shelf=othereuro-lit">othereuro-lit</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer819262907" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13065253167406001511">Although I somewhat enjoyed the experience of reading this novel, I found a lot lacking from it. I felt that the narrator was mostly a device that only worked moderately well. I found his voice grating, and the storytelling almost frantic, there is so much of it. But most of it, and its characters, didn’t interest me. Compared to Agnon, whose minor novel I just read, Tanpinar’s way of telling stories, at least in this translation, leaves much to be desired.<br/><br/>The novel is entertaining, but not, to </span>
  <span id="freeText13065253167406001511" style="display:none">Although I somewhat enjoyed the experience of reading this novel, I found a lot lacking from it. I felt that the narrator was mostly a device that only worked moderately well. I found his voice grating, and the storytelling almost frantic, there is so much of it. But most of it, and its characters, didn’t interest me. Compared to Agnon, whose minor novel I just read, Tanpinar’s way of telling stories, at least in this translation, leaves much to be desired.<br /><br />The novel is entertaining, but not, to me, funny. It lacks creativity, the edge of good satire, and interest (in some ways because I don’t know the culture or history). The novel's greatest assets seem to be that it is pleasant and easy to read, and takes place in a different culture at an important period of time for it.<br /><br />If I were learning more about the culture and history, or if I enjoyed (or were challenged by) the style and the storytelling, this novel is good enough for me to keep reading. But, at least in the first hundred pages, the narrator’s little world seems to have little to do with the culture or history, and I feel I’ve had a sufficient taste of the style and storytelling.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13065253167406001511" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/519606-tara?shelf=book-club">book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/519606-tara?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/519606-tara?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1078107916" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11502540928581126736">"The path to well-being springs from a sound understanding of time".<br/>So it is unfortunate that I was reading this book to meet a deadline, and couldn't afford to indulge a more relaxed reading.<br/>Knowing nothing of Turkish culture, I was unable to see the delicate nuances of this translated text. I'm sure there was some wordplay that I missed. However, I don't think that affected my overall enjoyment.<br/>I often drew comparisons to Lawrence Durrell's Alexandria Quartet, with their story told through con</span>
  <span id="freeText11502540928581126736" style="display:none">"The path to well-being springs from a sound understanding of time".<br />So it is unfortunate that I was reading this book to meet a deadline, and couldn't afford to indulge a more relaxed reading.<br />Knowing nothing of Turkish culture, I was unable to see the delicate nuances of this translated text. I'm sure there was some wordplay that I missed. However, I don't think that affected my overall enjoyment.<br />I often drew comparisons to Lawrence Durrell's Alexandria Quartet, with their story told through conversation and idea; with the drawn-out meaninglessness of a chapter. You don't really end up caring for the characters or their actions, you don't understand them. But you watch them as they wander through their inane lives.<br />It also paints a remarkable parallel to the Australian public service. Like Hayri Irdal, I too have felt the complete bafflement when one's sarcasm is received as a remarkable new initiative.<br />Had I wallowed in this text more thoroughly, I suspect it would earn a full five stars. It is definitely one I will be reading again.<br /></span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1325020942" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7331672396564388368">If you want to read a great Turkish novel i highly recommend this book as a perfect example of Turkish literature. Ahmet Hamdi Tanpınar's observation skills are marvellous. You will have an understanding of Turkish culture and how it is stuck between the east and the west. A very funny and weird story which perfectly criticises the community.<br/>As a native Turkish speaker i am only concerned about the translation. The author's style can be lost in translation so the readers should be very careful w</span>
  <span id="freeText7331672396564388368" style="display:none">If you want to read a great Turkish novel i highly recommend this book as a perfect example of Turkish literature. Ahmet Hamdi Tanpınar's observation skills are marvellous. You will have an understanding of Turkish culture and how it is stuck between the east and the west. A very funny and weird story which perfectly criticises the community.<br />As a native Turkish speaker i am only concerned about the translation. The author's style can be lost in translation so the readers should be very careful when it comes to choosing the translators, publishers.</span>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1700923437" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14593473798444825262">Such a great book. Some major kudos to the translators, who had to inhabit Tanpinar's mind in order to make the primarily contextual translations of Turkish. Given it a 5, as it's a perfect illustration of the culture of Istanbul; an eternal heartbeat between east and west, a constant flow of energy between the two; oxygenating all of humanity, from the illustrious to the inane.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('/rQ+TEEZ04X+ilyEBNgMYj0yd20ue78BAOnJcDhVUvrAEcikXUgdaSFr2oqEULLvbBo2D9WHv6KblIDHev+R2w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Ygc1C3E9bmNQG16xCslMeVIXryQ6227e11qBl0LT7o1cosPjbWygj4/62L+KQfL0Az/uRsEnbn1MJ8ggAHktrA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/2936248.Ahmet_Hamdi_Tanp_nar">About Ahmet Hamdi Tanpınar</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/2936248.Ahmet_Hamdi_Tanp_nar"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1475506085p3/2936248.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/2936248.Ahmet_Hamdi_Tanp_nar">Ahmet Hamdi Tanpınar
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
553
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14448832299824551004"><b>Ahmet Hamdi Tanpınar</b> (23 June 1901 - 24 January 1962) was one of the most important modern novelists and essayists of Turkish literature. He was also a member of the Turkish parliament (the Grand National Assembly of Turkey) between 1942 and 1946.<br/><br/>Tanpınar was born in Istanbul on 23 June 1901. His father was a judge, Hüseyin Fikri Efendi. Hüseyin Fikri Efendi was Georgian from Maçahel. Tanpınar's m</span>
  <span id="freeText14448832299824551004" style="display:none"><b>Ahmet Hamdi Tanpınar</b> (23 June 1901 - 24 January 1962) was one of the most important modern novelists and essayists of Turkish literature. He was also a member of the Turkish parliament (the Grand National Assembly of Turkey) between 1942 and 1946.<br /><br />Tanpınar was born in Istanbul on 23 June 1901. His father was a judge, Hüseyin Fikri Efendi. Hüseyin Fikri Efendi was Georgian from Maçahel. Tanpınar's mother died at Mosul, when Tanpınar was thirteen. Because his father's vocation required frequent relocation, Tanpınar continued his education in several different cities, including Istanbul, Sinop, Siirt, Kirkuk, and Antalya. After quitting veterinary college, he resumed his educational career at the Faculty of Literature at Istanbul University, which he completed in 1923. As a literature teacher, he taught at high schools in Erzurum (1923–1924), Konya, Ankara, the Educational Institute of Gazi and the Fine Arts Academy. At the Fine Arts Academy, besides teaching literature, Tanpınar taught in branches of aesthetics in arts, history of art and mythology (1932–1939). From 1942 to 1946, he entered the Turkish National Assembly as parliamentar of Kahramanmaraş. In 1953, he made an extensive journey to Europe, traveling many countries within six months such as France, Belgium, Holland, England, Spain and Italy. Tanpınar died of a heart attack on the 24 January 1962 in Istanbul. His grave is in the Aşiyan Graveyard, Istanbul.<br /><br />He is one of the most important authors of Turkish literature, successfully combining Eastern and Western cultures within his writings. Yahya Kemal Beyatlı played an important role in his upbringing. In his poetry, he uses Turkish classical music and dreaming as the textile of his works. Both in his poetry and novels psychological analyses, history, the characteristics of his time, the binding between the society and the individual, dreams and the problems of civilization are given a great place. One of his most significant works is The Time Regulation Institute (Saatleri Ayarlama Enstitüsü). The novel has been widely acclaimed as an ironic criticism of the bureaucratization process with the implication that its title suggests, though that is not what the book is all about. In fact, the book can be read from quite different perspectives, and cannot be exhausted in only one reading. First of all, it is a great psychological analysis of a man who suffers from being unable to adapt himself to his time, in other words to modern times. So the fact of bureaucratization is indeed incorporated into a broader problem: modernization and its impact on the individual. Most of the characters of the novel seem to be struggling in strange ways in order to survive in modern times. In this way, the concept of "time" occupies a central place, giving a deeper sense, even a philosophical taste to the novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14448832299824551004" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
